## User (2026-02-17T12:28:51.033Z)

i need to develop a complex way of prompting an agent. i want to be able to search scientific literature to come up with explanations for experiment data. i have a notion that somehow graphs of trees of references of a number of papers could somehow be turned into consensus conclusions, outlying conclusions and open questions.

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

This is a fascinating idea. The citation graph of scientific papers genuinely encodes epistemological structure — consensus, disagreement, and frontier. Let me sketch out how this could work as an agent system.

## Core Architecture

**Phase 1: Seed & Expand**
- Start with your experiment data + a natural language description of what you're investigating
- Agent queries literature APIs (Semantic Scholar, OpenAlex, PubMed) to find relevant seed papers
- From seeds, crawl the citation graph both directions — references (what they cite) and citations (who cites them)

**Phase 2: Claim Extraction**
- For each paper, the agent extracts structured claims: hypothesis, methods, key findings, stated limitations
- Each claim is tagged with metadata: paper ID, year, journal, citation count, position in the graph

**Phase 3: Graph Analysis — this is where your key insight lives**

The reference graph topology reveals epistemic status:

- **Consensus**: Claims that appear across multiple independent citation clusters. If papers that don't cite each other arrive at the same conclusion, that's strong convergence. Look for claims supported by nodes with high betweenness centrality.
- **Outliers**: Claims from papers that are structurally isolated — low co-citation coupling with the main cluster, or papers that are highly cited *despite* contradicting the dominant cluster (controversial but impactful).
- **Open questions**: Detectable by several signals — explicit "future work" statements, areas where citation chains terminate (recent papers with few citations that propose novel mechanisms), and zones where two well-cited clusters contradict each other without resolution.

**Phase 4: Synthesis against your data**
- Map your experimental observations against the extracted claim landscape
- Agent reports: "Your data is consistent with consensus X, potentially explained by mechanism Y, but contradicts outlier claim Z. Open question Q is directly testable by your setup."

## Key Graph Metrics That Map to Epistemology

| Graph Property | Epistemic Meaning |
|---|---|
| Co-citation clustering | Schools of thought / paradigms |
| Bibliographic coupling | Shared intellectual foundation |
| Bridge nodes (high betweenness) | Synthesis/review papers that connect paradigms |
| Orphan nodes with high degree | Controversial or paradigm-shifting work |
| Temporal citation decay | Whether a claim is still active or superseded |
| Citation sentiment (citing to agree vs. refute) | Support vs. challenge |

## The Hard Parts

1. **Citation sentiment** — a paper citing another might be agreeing, disagreeing, or just using it as background. This requires the LLM to read the citing context, not just note the edge exists.
2. **Claim deduplication** — the same finding stated differently across 50 papers needs to be recognized as one claim.
3. **Scale** — a real literature graph can have thousands of nodes. You need pruning heuristics (relevance scoring, recency weighting).

---

Want to move toward implementation? If so, I'd want to understand:

- What domain is your experiment data in?
- Do you have API access to any literature databases (Semantic Scholar is free, OpenAlex is free)?
- Is this meant to run as a CLI tool, a notebook, a web app?
- What language/stack do you prefer?

This is ambitious but very buildable — the pieces (literature APIs, graph libraries, LLM claim extraction) all exist, the novel part is the graph-to-epistemology mapping you're describing.

# Recursive Language Models - Paper Summary

## Title
**Recursive Language Models**

## Authors
Alex L. Zhang, Tim Kraska, Omar Khattab

## Overview
This paper presents a novel approach to extending language model capabilities through recursive processing, enabling models to handle complex reasoning and long-horizon tasks more effectively.

## Key Contributions

The research introduces a framework where language models engage in iterative, self-referential computation. Rather than processing inputs linearly, the system allows models to "call themselves" recursively, creating hierarchical reasoning structures that can tackle problems requiring multiple stages of analysis.

## Main Methods

The approach employs recursive decomposition where models break complex tasks into subtasks, solving each through recursive calls. This enables:

- **Hierarchical problem-solving**: Large problems decompose into manageable subproblems
- **Iterative refinement**: Solutions improve through recursive feedback loops
- **Context management**: Careful handling of context across recursive layers prevents information loss
- **Structured reasoning**: Explicit decomposition creates interpretable solution paths

## Results

The framework demonstrates improvements on:

- Long-context comprehension tasks
- Complex reasoning benchmarks (LongBench, BooookScore)
- Multi-step problem solving
- Agent-based tasks requiring extended reasoning horizons

Performance scaling shows consistent gains as recursion depth increases, though optimal depths vary by task complexity.

## Practical Applications

- **Code generation**: Enhanced multi-file code synthesis
- **Document analysis**: Improved processing of lengthy texts
- **Long-horizon planning**: Better agent navigation of extended state spaces
- **Information synthesis**: Superior summarization of complex documents

## Conclusions

Recursive language models provide a principled mechanism for extending model reasoning capabilities beyond single-pass processing. The architecture offers both performance improvements and enhanced interpretability through explicit decomposition structures.

# Paper Summary: Recursive Language Models

**Title:** Recursive Language Models

**Authors:** Alex L. Zhang, Tim Kraska, Omar Khattab

**arXiv ID:** 2512.24601

**Submission Date:** December 31, 2025 (v1); January 28, 2026 (v2)

**Subject Areas:** Artificial Intelligence (cs.AI); Computation and Language (cs.CL)

## Abstract

The researchers investigate enabling large language models to handle exceptionally long prompts through inference-time scaling techniques. They introduce Recursive Language Models (RLMs), an inference framework where LLMs can "programmatically examine, decompose, and recursively call itself over snippets of the prompt." 

Key findings include:
- RLMs successfully process inputs up to "two orders of magnitude beyond model context windows"
- Performance substantially exceeds standard frontier LLMs across four long-context tasks with comparable computational cost
- A post-trained small-scale model, RLM-Qwen3-8B, achieves "28.3% improvement on average" over the base Qwen3-8B and approaches GPT-5 quality on several benchmarks

**Availability:** Code is provided at the authors' GitHub repository.

**Paper Length:** 9 pages (33 pages with appendix)

**License:** CC BY 4.0

```
# Recursive Language Models: Detailed Technical Summary

## 1. Problem Statement

Current frontier LLMs face two critical limitations when processing long contexts:

- **Context window constraints**: Models have fixed maximum input lengths (e.g., GPT-5 has ~272K tokens). Beyond this, they simply cannot process information.
- **Context rot**: "Quality degrades steeply as prompts get longer" even within context limits. Performance degradation accelerates with task complexity—simple needle-in-haystack tasks degrade slowly, while tasks requiring dense information processing fail catastrophically.

The authors note that while models could theoretically benefit from longer training, they investigate whether inference-time modifications can achieve orders-of-magnitude improvements in effective context handling.

## 2. Core Mechanistic Innovation

RLMs treat the long prompt as an **external object** rather than feeding it into the neural network directly. The key insight: "arbitrarily long user prompts should not be fed into the neural network directly but should instead be treated as part of the environment that the LLM is tasked to symbolically and recursively interact with."

**Concrete workflow**:
- Initialize a Python REPL with the long prompt stored as a string variable
- Provide the LLM with only metadata: prompt length, short prefix, access instructions
- The LLM writes Python code to examine, chunk, and process the prompt
- The LLM invokes itself recursively on code-generated chunks via an `llm_query()` function
- Only metadata about code outputs (not full outputs) enters the LLM's context window
- Final answer stored in a REPL variable, then returned

## 3. Algorithm Specification

**Algorithm 1 (RLM Loop)**:

```
Input: prompt P
Output: response Y

1. state ← InitREPL(prompt=P)
2. state ← AddFunction(state, sub_RLM)
3. hist ← [Metadata(state)]

while True:
    code ← LLM(hist)
    (state, stdout) ← REPL(state, code)
    hist ← hist ‖ code ‖ Metadata(stdout)
    
    if state[Final] is set:
        return state[Final]
```

**Three critical design choices distinguishing RLMs from inferior alternatives**:

1. **Symbolic handle to prompt**: The prompt exists as a REPL variable, not in the LLM context. This prevents context window limitations from immediately constraining input size.

2. **Programmatic output generation**: Rather than autoregressive generation, final answers are constructed as REPL variables. This enables "unbounded output tokens" by avoiding the model's output token limit.

3. **Symbolic recursion inside code**: The executed code (inside the REPL) can invoke `llm_query()` in loops over sliced prompt data. This permits "Ω(|P|) or Ω(|P|²) semantic work"—the number of LLM calls scales with input complexity, not bounded by a single call.

**Decomposition strategies observed** (emergent, not hand-engineered):
- Uniform chunking by newlines
- Keyword-based filtering using regex to narrow search space
- Iterative accumulation of results 
... (truncated)
```
</details>

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

## Recursive Language Models (RLMs) — Zhang, Kraska, Khattab (Dec 2025)

### The Problem

LLMs have two walls when dealing with long inputs:
1. **Hard wall**: Context windows have a maximum (e.g. ~272K tokens for GPT-5). Beyond that, nothing works.
2. **Soft wall** ("context rot"): Even *within* the window, quality degrades steeply as input gets longer — and the degradation is **worse for harder tasks**. A needle-in-a-haystack lookup barely suffers, but tasks requiring dense information processing (aggregation, pairwise comparison) collapse.

### The Core Idea

**Don't feed the long prompt into the model. Give the model a *handle* to it instead.**

The prompt is stored as a string variable in a Python REPL. The LLM receives only metadata (length, a short prefix, instructions for accessing it). Then the LLM **writes Python code** to:
1. Slice, chunk, filter, or regex-search the prompt
2. Call `llm_query()` on each chunk — invoking *itself* on manageable pieces
3. Accumulate sub-results in REPL variables
4. Produce a final answer stored in a REPL variable (bypassing output token limits too)

The model never sees the full input in its context window. It *programs its own reading strategy*.

### Why This Is Powerful

The key distinction is **semantic work scaling**. A single LLM call does O(1) semantic work regardless of input length. RLMs let the model issue O(n) or even O(n²) LLM calls via loops in code, matching the actual complexity of the task. The decomposition strategies are **emergent** — not hand-coded. The model figures out on its own whether to chunk by newlines, filter by regex, or do pairwise comparisons.

### Results

| Task | Complexity | Base GPT-5 | RLM(GPT-5) | Improvement |
|------|-----------|------------|-------------|-------------|
| S-NIAH (needle search) | O(1) | ~95% | ~95% | Comparable |
| BrowseComp-Plus (multi-hop QA, 6-11M tokens) | O(n) | 0.0% | **91.3%** | ∞ (impossible without RLM) |
| OOLONG (aggregation, 131K tokens) | O(n) | 44.0% | **56.5%** | +28% |
| OOLONG-Pairs (pairwise, 32K tokens) | O(n²) | 0.1% | **58.0%** | 580x |

The harder the task and the longer the input, the bigger the win. Cost is comparable to or cheaper than alternatives.

### Small Model Fine-Tuning

They fine-tuned Qwen3-8B on just ~1,000 training samples (RLM trajectories generated by a larger model on an *unrelated* domain). With 48 H100-hours of training, the 8B model gained **28.3% average improvement** and approached GPT-5 quality on several benchmarks. The RLM reasoning pattern transferred across domains.

### Relevance to Your Project

This paper is directly relevant to the literature analysis system you described. The RLM pattern — "give the agent a handle to a large dataset, let it programmatically decompose and recursively query itself" — is exactly how you'd want an agent to process a large citation graph or corpus of papers. Instead of stuffing 500 papers into context, the agent would:

1. Hold the paper corpus and citation graph as REPL variables
2. Write code to traverse the graph, identify clusters, select relevant papers
3. Call `llm_query()` on individual papers or clusters for claim extraction
4. Accumulate and synthesize results programmatically

The emergent decomposition strategies they observed (chunking, filtering, iterative accumulation) map directly to literature analysis operations (clustering by citation community, filtering by relevance, accumulating claims by topic).

The Molecular Signatures Database (MSigDB) is one of the most widely used and comprehensive databases of gene sets for performing gene set enrichment analysis. A collection of "hallmark" gene sets was developed as part of MSigDB using a combination of automated approaches and expert curation. Each hallmark consists of a "refined" gene set, derived from multiple "founder" sets, that conveys a specific biological state or process and displays coherent expression.

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

Here's what I found. This is a rich field with several clear entry points.

## Anchor Paper (Start Here)

**[Progress in toxicogenomics to protect human health](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41576-024-00767-1)** — Meier et al., *Nature Reviews Genetics*, Feb 2025. This is the most recent comprehensive review. Covers transcriptomic biomarkers, network inference, dose-response modelling, single-cell transcriptomics, and multi-omics. This paper's reference list alone would seed a massive citation graph.

## Key Organ-Specific Resources

**Liver (most developed):**
- [TXG-MAP](https://www.nature.com/articles/tpj201717) — 415 co-expression modules from 654 DrugMatrix rat liver experiments, organized as a visual dendrogram. This is essentially a pre-built "hallmark" map for liver toxicogenomics.
- [Human hepatocyte TXG-MAPr](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s00204-021-03141-w) — The human counterpart, enabling cross-species comparison.
- [Six gene expression biomarkers for rat liver tumorigens](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC8026143/) — Tested across 2,000+ comparisons.
- 12 marker genes for genotoxic hepatocarcinogens: Aen, Bax, Btg2, Ccnf, Ccng1, Cdkn1a, Gdf15, Lrp1, Mbd1, Phlda3, Plk2, Tubb4b.

**Kidney:**
- [Gene co-expression networks with rat kidney](https://www.biorxiv.org/content/10.1101/2023.11.03.565444v2.full.pdf) — Kidney-specific WGCNA modules analogous to liver TXG-MAP.
- Known kidney biomarker genes: KIM1/HAVCR1, LCN2, SLC22A6.

**Multi-organ (liver + kidney + more):**
- [TransTox](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41746-024-01317-z) — GAN-based model that translates transcriptomic profiles *between* organs (liver ↔ kidney). Very recent (2024).
- [Literature-optimized organ-specific gene sets](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6331104/) — 48 genes for liver, 21 for kidney, selected for rat-human ortholog conservation.
- Early response consensus: EGR1, ATF3, GDF15, FGF21 — induced 2h post-exposure in both human and rat hepatocytes.

## Major Databases

| Database | What it contains | Access |
|----------|-----------------|--------|
| [Open TG-GATEs](https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/25313160/) | 170 compounds, rat in vivo (liver, kidney, heart, muscle) + in vitro hepatocytes, 20K+ microarray assays | Free |
| [DrugMatrix](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6080592/) | 654 rat liver experiments, gene expression + pathology | Free |
| [CTD](https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/36169237/) | 45M toxicogenomic relationships, 16K chemicals, 51K genes, anatomy-based browsing | Free |
| [MSigDB Hallmark Gene Sets](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC4707969/) | 50 hallmark sets (p53, NF-kB, oxidative stress, etc.), validated in rat data, ortholog mappable | Free |
| [Reconciled rat-human metabolic networks](https://www.nature.com/articles/ncomms14250) | Genome-scale metabolic reconstructions for comparative toxicogenomics | Free |

## On Your REPL Question

REPL is relevant **right now**, not just later. Here's why:

The Meier et al. 2025 review alone has probably 100-200 references. You could immediately use an RLM-style approach to:

1. **Fetch the paper** and its reference list programmatically (Semantic Scholar API gives you structured citation data for free)
2. **Expand the citation graph** — for each reference, pull its citations and references
3. **Cluster by organ** — use the LLM to classify each paper by target organ(s)
4. **Extract hallmark gene lists** — for each paper, extract any named gene sets or biomarkers
5. **Build consensus** — genes that appear across multiple independent papers/clusters = established hallmark genes; genes that appear in only one cluster = candidate novel biomarkers

The alternative — manually reading 200 papers — is exactly the kind of task that RLM-style decomposition was designed for. The REPL holds the growing citation graph and gene lists as data structures while the LLM recursively queries itself on individual papers.

Want me to start building this? The natural first step would be a Python script that hits the Semantic Scholar API with the Meier 2025 paper, pulls its reference graph, and structures it for LLM-driven analysis.

## User (2026-02-17T12:44:28.058Z)

please do. i'm thinking that this sort of thing should have a "when to stop" governor. am i right?

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

You're absolutely right. Without a governor, the citation graph explodes exponentially — a review with 150 references, each citing 50 papers, is already 7,500 nodes at depth 2. And most of those will be irrelevant (methods papers, tangential biology, etc.).

The governor needs multiple stopping dimensions:

1. **Depth** — max hops from seed papers
2. **Breadth** — max papers per expansion
3. **Relevance decay** — score each paper against the core topic, stop expanding below a threshold
4. **Saturation** — detect when new papers stop contributing new genes/concepts
5. **Budget** — hard cap on total papers and API calls

Let me build this.

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

Smart thinking. The citation graph crawl itself doesn't use any LLM tokens — it's pure API calls to Semantic Scholar. But the *next* phase (claim extraction, gene identification, consensus synthesis from paper texts) is where token costs would explode.

Here's how we can split the work:

**Semantic Scholar crawl** (what we just built) — zero LLM tokens, just HTTP requests. Can run now.

**LLM-heavy tasks** (do locally with Ollama):
- Relevance scoring with actual comprehension (upgrade from keyword matching)
- Claim extraction from abstracts/full texts
- Gene list extraction from papers
- Consensus/outlier/open-question synthesis

Let me check what you have available.

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

Good setup. You have:
- Ollama 0.16.1 installed
- RTX 3070 Ti (8GB VRAM)
- qwen2.5:7b already pulled (fits in 8GB)

With 8GB VRAM, you can comfortably run 7-8B parameter models. Qwen2.5:7b is actually a solid choice for structured extraction tasks. For this project, here's what I'd recommend:

| Task | Model | Why |
|------|-------|-----|
| Claim extraction from abstracts | qwen2.5:7b (already have it) | Good at following structured output instructions |
| Gene name extraction | qwen2.5:7b | Pattern recognition task, doesn't need huge model |
| Relevance scoring (upgraded) | qwen2.5:7b | Simple classification |
| Final consensus synthesis | Opus (this session) | This is the hard part — needs strong reasoning across hundreds of claims |

So the flow would be:
1. **Crawl** (no LLM) — Semantic Scholar API, build graph
2. **Extract** (local Ollama) — for each paper's abstract, extract claims, genes, organ relevance
3. **Synthesize** (Opus) — with the structured extractions in hand, one pass to identify consensus, outliers, open questions

This means Opus only sees the *distilled output* — maybe a few thousand tokens of structured claims — instead of hundreds of paper abstracts.

Want me to wire Ollama into the pipeline? I'd add an extraction module that hits `localhost:11434` for the bulk work.

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

I need to add `OLLAMA_HOST=0.0.0.0` so it listens on all interfaces. This requires editing a systemd service file with sudo, so you'll need to run this on the remote machine:

```bash

```

This creates a drop-in override that tells Ollama to listen on all interfaces (port 11434), then restarts the service. Let me know when that's done and I'll verify connectivity.

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

This confirms what I suspected — **abstracts alone are too sparse for robust gene extraction**. Only 51 unique genes surfaced, with PPARα as the sole "consensus" gene at 3+ papers. The real gene lists are buried in full-text methods/results sections.

Here's where we stand and what I'd recommend next:

## What We Have
- **150 papers** in a structured citation graph (GML format, loadable in Gephi)
- **151 citation edges** showing the reference network
- **47 abstract-level extractions** with claims, organs, methods
- Organ distribution mapped, initial gene signals identified

## What's Limiting Us
Abstract-only extraction misses ~90% of the gene mentions. The real hallmark gene lists are in tables, supplementary materials, and results sections of papers.

## Three Paths Forward

1. **Full-text extraction** — Use Semantic Scholar's open-access full text API or fetch PDFs from PubMed Central for the top papers, then run those through the 14b model. Much richer gene extraction.

2. **Database-first approach** — Instead of mining papers for genes, pull curated gene sets directly from CTD, MSigDB, or the TXG-MAPr tool, then use the citation graph to validate and contextualize them.

3. **Expand the crawl** — Run with `max_papers=500` and `max_depth=3` to get more papers, then re-extract. More data = better consensus signal even from abstracts.

Which direction interests you most? Or a combination?

## User (2026-02-17T13:26:08.314Z)

do you reckon it's worth adding gpus? can we divide and conquer?

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

Absolutely, and your hardware fleet is well-suited for it. But let me be honest about where parallelism actually helps vs. where it doesn't:

**Where more GPUs help:**
- Full-text extraction (if we go that route) — each paper is 5-20K tokens, 500 papers = real time
- Running the larger crawl (500+ papers) through extraction
- Running *different* models simultaneously for cross-validation

**Where they don't help:**
- The Semantic Scholar crawl — bottlenecked by API rate limits, not compute
- The consensus analysis — pure Python, instant

Here's what I'd set up:

| Machine | Model | Role | Estimated speed |
|---------|-------|------|----------------|
| Remote 6900XT (16GB) | qwen2.5:14b | Primary extractor — best quality | ~15 tok/s |
| Mac Mini 48GB | qwen2.5:32b | Deep extraction — highest quality | ~10 tok/s |
| Local 3070 Ti (8GB) | qwen2.5:7b | Fast pass — relevance scoring, filtering | ~30 tok/s |
| Mac Mini 24GB | qwen2.5:14b | Parallel extractor #2 | ~15 tok/s |

The 48GB Mac Mini is the interesting one — it can run a 32B model, which would give meaningfully better extraction quality for the hard cases (disambiguating gene names, understanding complex claims).

The code change is straightforward — instead of a single primary endpoint, the extraction engine would round-robin across a pool of endpoints.

Want me to set this up? I'd need:
1. SSH access details for the Mac Minis you want to use
2. ~10 minutes to install Ollama on each and pull models

Or if you want to test the value first, we could just add the 48GB Mac Mini with the 32B model and see if extraction quality improves before scaling wider.

## User (2026-02-17T13:28:04.669Z)

is the semantic scholar crawl a one-time activity? should we do a big one up front, and then do increasingly larger scale analyses?

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

Yes — that's exactly the right architecture. The crawl is essentially building a **local corpus** that you then mine repeatedly with increasingly sophisticated analyses. Here's why:

**The citation graph is mostly static.** A paper's references never change. Its citation count grows, and new papers appear that cite it, but the core structure is stable. So a big upfront crawl gives you a durable asset.

**The analysis phases are where iteration lives.** You'd run many different extraction prompts, try different gene identification strategies, cluster the graph different ways — all against the same corpus.

So the strategy would be:

```
Phase 1: BIG CRAWL (one-time, no GPU needed)
   │  Semantic Scholar API, rate-limited, ~2 hours for 1000 papers
   │  Output: papers.json, edges.json, citation_graph.gml
   │
   ▼
Phase 2: EXTRACT (GPU fleet, repeatable)
   │  Run different extraction prompts against the corpus
   │  Try different models (7b for speed, 32b for quality)
   │  Iterate on what to extract (genes, claims, methods, pathways)
   │
   ▼
Phase 3: SYNTHESIZE (GPU + possibly Opus for final reasoning)
   │  Consensus/outlier/open-question analysis
   │  Organ-specific hallmark gene sets
   │  Cross-validate against curated databases
   │
   ▼
Phase 4: REFRESH (periodic, incremental)
      Only fetch new citations of existing papers
      Much cheaper than full re-crawl
```

For the big crawl, I'd recommend:
- **max_papers=800**, **max_depth=3**
- That'll take ~2 hours due to rate limiting
- We could also get a free [Semantic Scholar API key](https://www.semanticscholar.org/product/api#api-key) which raises the limit from ~1 req/s to ~10 req/s — cutting it to ~12 minutes

Want me to kick off the big crawl now? It can run in the background overnight if needed, and then we set up the GPU fleet for the analysis phase where parallelism actually pays off.

## User (2026-02-17T13:29:18.763Z)

what if we could do the crawl from multiple locations, and put it back together after? 

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

Smart thinking. Semantic Scholar rate-limits **per IP address**. So 6 machines = 6x throughput. The crawl doesn't need GPUs at all — just Python and an internet connection. Every machine in your fleet can participate.

The design:

1. **Seed from one machine** — establish the initial papers and build the expansion queue
2. **Export the queue** — list of paper IDs that need their refs/cites fetched
3. **Split across machines** — each machine gets a chunk of IDs to expand
4. **Each worker fetches independently** — saves results as JSON
5. **Merge back** — combine all worker outputs into one unified graph

```
         ┌─────────┐
         │  Seed   │  (one machine, establishes starting papers)
         └────┬────┘
              │ queue of ~200 paper IDs to expand
    ┌─────────┼─────────┬──────────┬──────────┐
    ▼         ▼         ▼          ▼          ▼
 local    amd-remote  mini-48   mini-24    mini-16
 ~40 IDs   ~40 IDs   ~40 IDs   ~40 IDs    ~40 IDs
    │         │         │          │          │
    ▼         ▼         ▼          ▼          ▼
 results   results   results   results    results
    └─────────┼─────────┴──────────┴──────────┘
              ▼
         ┌─────────┐
         │  Merge  │  → unified graph + deduped papers
         └─────────┘
```

Each worker only needs `requests` — no special deps. I can package it as a single self-contained Python script that takes a JSON file of paper IDs and outputs a JSON file of results.

With 6 machines at ~1 req/s each, an 800-paper crawl (~2400 API calls) drops from **~2 hours to ~20 minutes**.

Want me to build the distributed crawler? I'd need SSH access to the Mac Minis, or you could just `scp` the worker script to each one and run it manually.

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)



Just web searching — zero GPU involvement. The crawl is pure HTTP requests to the Semantic Scholar API. Any machine with Python and an internet connection can do it.

So right now we can run from two IPs: this machine and the AMD remote. That's already 2x throughput, cutting the big crawl from ~2 hours to ~1 hour. Want me to build the distributed worker and kick it off on both machines?

## User (2026-02-17T13:31:47.329Z)

we won't get throttled because the wan ip is the same for all?

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)



You're right — if both machines are behind the same router, Semantic Scholar sees the same public IP. No benefit from splitting across LAN machines.

The Mac Minis would only help if they're on a different network (different WAN IP). Otherwise it's all the same rate limit.

So for the crawl, our options are:

1. **Just run it from here** — ~2 hours for 800 papers, fire and forget
2. **Get a free Semantic Scholar API key** — raises the limit to ~10 req/s, bringing it down to ~12 minutes
3. **Use a VPN/proxy on one machine** — different exit IP, but probably not worth the complexity

Option 2 is by far the easiest win. It's a free signup at [semanticscholar.org/product/api](https://www.semanticscholar.org/product/api#api-key). Want to grab a key, or should I just kick off the big crawl now and let it run?

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

Yes — real 2x speedup. The extraction is embarrassingly parallel: each paper is independent. Split the 798 papers in half, each machine runs qwen2.5:14b on its chunk, merge results.

With one machine processing ~15 papers/minute, 798 papers takes ~53 minutes. With two machines, ~27 minutes.

The only question is whether it's worth the setup time. If you're planning to iterate on prompts (which you will — trying different extraction strategies, different questions), the parallel infrastructure pays for itself on the second run.

Want me to pull the 14b model on both machines and set up the parallel extraction?

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

There's the full picture. **32 consensus genes** (3+ papers) and **41 moderate-evidence genes** (2 papers), plus 388 single mentions.

Some patterns worth noting:

**Cross-organ stress response core:** NRF2, BAX, P53/TP53, BCL-2, Caspase-3, NF-κB — these appear across nearly every organ. They're the universal toxicity alarm system (oxidative stress, apoptosis, inflammation).

**Liver-specific:** PPARα (12), AHR (4), CAR (3), CYP1A1 (3), CYP7A1 (3), GCLC (3), HSP90 (3), plus moderate hits like CYP2E1, BSEP, PXR, FXR — classic xenobiotic metabolism and bile acid transport.

**Kidney-emerging:** CLU (2), KIM-1 (2), CD44 (2), MDM2 (2) — nephrotoxicity-specific markers showing up alongside the universal stress genes.

**Heart:** MFN2 (3), SIRT1 (3), IL-1β (3), AMPK (2), TTN (2), DRP1 (2) — mitochondrial dynamics and energy sensing, which makes sense for cardiac tissue.

**Notable duplicates to reconcile:** P53 (8) and TP53 (6) are the same gene. BCL-2 (7) and BCL2 (4) likewise. TNF-α (4) and TNFΑ (2) and TNF (2). Merging these would boost their rankings significantly. Want me to add a gene name normalization step?

<details><summary>Tool output (1360 chars)</summary>

```
   389→# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
   390→# Gene consensus analysis
   391→# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
   392→
   393→def analyze_gene_consensus(extractions_file: str = "citegraph_output/extractions.json"):
   394→    """
   395→    Analyze extracted genes to find:
   396→    - Consensus hallmark genes (appear in many papers, across methods)
   397→    - Organ-specific gene sets
   398→    - Outlier/novel genes (few mentions but high confidence)
   399→    """
   400→    with open(extractions_file) as f:
   401→        extractions = json.load(f)
   402→
   403→    # Build gene-to-papers mapping
   404→    gene_papers: dict[str, list[dict]] = {}
   405→    gene_organs: dict[str, set] = {}
   406→
   407→    for ext in extractions:
   408→        genes = ext.get("genes", [])
   409→        organs = ext.get("organs", [])
   410→        for gene in genes:
   411→            if gene not in gene_papers:
   412→                gene_papers[gene] = []
   413→                gene_organs[gene] = set()
   414→            gene_papers[gene].append({
   415→                "paper_id": ext["paper_id"],
   416→                "title": ext["title"],
   417→                "year": ext.get("year"),
   418→                "confidence": ext.get("confidence", 0),
```
</details>

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

> **Bash** — Analyze single-mention genes grouped by organ with confidence
> ```
> uv run python3 -c "
import json

data = json.load(open('citegraph_output/gene_consensus.json'))
extractions = json.load(open('citegraph_output_800/extractions.json'))

# Rebuild single-mention genes with full context
from collections import defaultdict

# Gene alias normalization (inline for this script)
GENE_ALIASES = {
    'P53': 'TP53', 'BCL-2': 'BCL2', 'CASPASE-3': 'CASP3', 'TNF-Α': 'TNF',
    'TNFΑ': 'TNF', 'NF-ΚB': 'NFKB1', 'NRF2': 'NFE2L2', 'PPARΑ': 'PPARA',
    'IL-6': 'IL6', 'IL-1Β': 'IL1B', 'NRF1': 'NFE2L1', 'HIF-1Α': 'HIF1A',
    'KIM-1': 'HAVCR1', 'KIM1': 'HAVCR1', 'VEGF': 'VEGFA', 'TGF-Β': 'TGFB1',
    'HSP90': 'HSP90AA1', 'DRP1': 'DNM1L', 'COX-2': 'PTGS2', 'P62': 'SQSTM1',
    'AKT': 'AKT1', 'ERK': 'MAPK1', 'JNK': 'MAPK8', 'AMPK': 'PRKAA1',
    'PGC1Α': 'PPARGC1A', 'DJ-1/PARK7': 'PARK7', 'HMOX-1': 'HMOX1',
    'NF-KAPPAB': 'NFKB1', 'RXRΑ': 'RXRA', 'FOXO3A': 'FOXO3',
    'CASPASE-9': 'CASP9', 'CASPASE-8': 'CASP8', 'BAK': 'BAK1',
    'TNF-ALPHA': 'TNF', 'IL-10': 'IL10', 'IL-33': 'IL33',
    'KEAP-1': 'KEAP1', 'BCL-XL': 'BCL2L1', 'NGAL': 'LCN2',
    'PI3K': 'PIK3CA', 'CYTOCHROME P450': 'CYP', 'TNFA': 'TNF',
    'P21': 'CDKN1A', 'SOD': 'SOD1', 'GPX': 'GPX1', 'BECLIN-1': 'BECN1',
    'HO-1': 'HMOX1', 'SIRT-1': 'SIRT1', 'FGF-21': 'FGF21',
    'GDF-15': 'GDF15', 'NFKB': 'NFKB1', 'NF-KB': 'NFKB1',
    'MFN-2': 'MFN2', 'CASPASE3': 'CASP3', 'CASPASE-1': 'CASP1',
    'CYP2E1': 'CYP2E1', 'MTOR': 'MTOR',
}

skip_patterns = ['NOT EXPLICITLY', 'KEY GENES', 'CORE GENES', 'GENE EXPRESSION',
                 'SIGNATURE', 'PATHWAY', 'TOTAL', 'IMPLIED', 'LISTED']

def norm(g):
    g = g.strip().upper()
    for p in skip_patterns:
        if p in g:
            return ''
    return GENE_ALIASES.get(g, g)

organ_map = {
    'hepatocytes': 'liver', 'kidneys': 'kidney', 'lungs': 'lung',
    'cerebellum': 'brain', 'hippocampus': 'brain', 'cortex': 'brain',
    'striatum': 'brain', 'nervous system': 'brain',
    'cardiovascular system': 'heart', 'pulmonary system': 'lung',
    'gastrointestinal tract': 'intestine', 'gut': 'intestine',
    'colon': 'intestine', 'rectum': 'intestine', 'esophagus': 'stomach',
    'gallbladder': 'liver', 'bile duct': 'liver', 'plasma': 'blood',
}

# Build single-mention list with paper context
gene_info = defaultdict(lambda: {'papers': [], 'organs': set()})
for ext in extractions:
    organs = {organ_map.get(o.lower(), o.lower()) for o in ext.get('organs', [])}
    for raw in ext.get('genes', []):
        g = norm(raw)
        if not g:
            continue
        gene_info[g]['papers'].append({
            'title': ext['title'],
            'year': ext.get('year'),
            'confidence': ext.get('confidence', 0),
            'relevance': ext.get('relevance_score', 0) if 'relevance_score' in ext else 0,
        })
        gene_info[g]['organs'].update(organs)

singles = {g: v for g, v in gene_info.items() if len(v['papers']) == 1}

# Sort by paper confidence, then filter for real gene symbols (2-10 chars, alphanumeric)
import re
real_genes = {}
for g, v in singles.items():
    if not re.match(r'^[A-Z][A-Z0-9]{1,9}$', g):
        continue
    real_genes[g] = v

# Sort by confidence
by_conf = sorted(real_genes.items(), key=lambda x: x[1]['papers'][0]['confidence'], reverse=True)

print(f'Total single-mention genes: {len(singles)}')
print(f'After filtering to real gene symbols: {len(real_genes)}')
print()

# Group by organ
organ_singles = defaultdict(list)
for g, v in real_genes.items():
    for organ in v['organs']:
        organ_singles[organ].append((g, v['papers'][0]))

print('SINGLE-MENTION GENES BY ORGAN (high confidence)')
print('='*90)
for organ in sorted(organ_singles.keys()):
    genes = organ_singles[organ]
    # Sort by confidence
    genes.sort(key=lambda x: x[1]['confidence'], reverse=True)
    high_conf = [(g, p) for g, p in genes if p['confidence'] >= 0.8]
    if high_conf:
        print(f'\n{organ.upper()} ({len(high_conf)} high-confidence singles):')
        for g, p in high_conf[:15]:
            print(f'  {g:<12} conf={p[\"confidence\"]:.2f}  {p[\"title\"][:65]}  ({p[\"year\"]})')
" 2>&1
> ```

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

Yes, there are several genuinely interesting outliers. Let me highlight the ones that stand out:

**Kidney — novel biomarker candidates:**
- **ATF3** (conf 0.95) — early-response gene, from the 2013 consensus signature paper. This is actually a strong candidate that's only "single" here because only one paper in our corpus names it explicitly for kidney (it's in the consensus list for liver)
- **ALG6** — from the KidneyNetwork study (2021), a prediction-based hit
- **CLU** (clusterin) — already at 2 mentions, a well-known renal injury marker that likely appears more in full texts

**Heart — strong emerging signals:**
- **PTN** (pleiotrophin, conf 0.95) — SIRT1 axis, oxidative stress protection, very recent (2026)
- **HTRA3, IGFBP7** (conf 0.95) — cardiac fibroblast regulators of heart failure
- **SLC2A3, AKR1C3, DAPK2, MAP3K8, TRIB1** (all conf 0.95) — from a 2025 single-cell sequencing study of doxorubicin cardiotoxicity. These are machine-learning-identified hub genes, exactly the kind of novel candidates you'd want to validate
- **OPA1** (conf 0.90) — mitochondrial fusion, counterpart to DRP1/MFN2 already in consensus

**Lung — a whole gene panel emerged:**
- **ACADL, PUMA, BIK, CAT, COX7A1, PAICS, PDK4, PRDX4, PYCR1, FIS1** (all conf 0.95) — all from a single 2026 integrated transcriptomic study. This looks like a promising lung toxicity signature that's too new to have citations yet

**Liver — high-value singles:**
- **HSPH1** (conf 1.0!) — the only perfect-confidence extraction, heat shock protein correlated with liver damage
- **ACAA2** (conf 0.95) — from the EGR1 hepatotoxicity paper
- **SPP1** (osteopontin, conf 0.95) — fibrosis marker, well-established but underrepresented in abstracts
- **SOCS3, TFDP1** (conf 0.90) — from causal regulatory network analysis of toxicogenomics data

**Blood/PBMC — potential circulating biomarkers:**
- The whole set from the co-expression network paper: **CDCP1, GSTA4, MAFB, TCF21, TGFBR3** — these are interesting because they're detectable in blood, making them candidates for non-invasive toxicity monitoring

The pattern is clear: many of these singles come from recent (2025-2026) papers using single-cell sequencing or ML-based network analysis — newer methods surfacing genes that the older microarray literature hasn't caught up with yet. These are exactly the "frontier" genes that would become consensus in 2-3 years. Worth watching, especially the heart and lung panels.

## User (2026-02-17T15:53:03.815Z)

correct me if i'm wrong, but wasn't our search for just liver an kidney? the others rode along in the results?

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)



You're right. The seed papers and search queries were focused on liver and kidney:

- 4 of 7 DOI seeds were liver-focused (TXG-MAP, TXG-MAPr, TG-GATEs, reconciled metabolic networks)
- 1 was liver+kidney (TransTox)
- 2 were general (Meier review, MSigDB hallmarks)
- The keyword searches were: "toxicogenomics rat **kidney** gene expression biomarkers", "toxicogenomics rat **heart** cardiac gene signatures", "toxicogenomics organ-specific hallmark genes rat human"

So heart got one explicit search, but brain, lung, intestine, etc. were never targeted — they just came along as references-of-references through the citation graph expansion. The 24 heart papers, 20 brain papers, and 4 lung papers are essentially incidental catches from the reference chains of liver/kidney papers.

This means two things:

1. **The liver/kidney results are relatively solid** — those organs had directed seeding and deep expansion
2. **The heart/brain/lung gene lists are undersampled** — what we found is real but there's much more out there. Those organs deserve their own targeted crawls with organ-specific seed papers and keywords

Want me to run dedicated crawls for the underrepresented organs? I'd add organ-specific seed papers (e.g. the 401-gene cardiac signature paper, the lung transcriptomic paper from 2026) and targeted search queries. The crawl infrastructure is already built — just needs different seeds.

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

So you have ~2,800 GO Biological Process terms with cluster assignments. To get member genes for each GO term, there are a few approaches:

**1. Gene Ontology API (free, no key needed)**
The QuickGO REST API from EBI returns annotated genes per GO term:
```
https://www.ebi.ac.uk/QuickGO/services/annotation/downloadSearch?goId=GO:0000018&taxonId=10116
```
(taxonId 10116 = rat, 9606 = human)

**2. GO OBO + GAF files (bulk download)**
Download the gene association file for rat from GO directly — one file, all terms mapped to genes. Much faster than 2,800 API calls.

**3. MSigDB C5 collection**
Already has GO BP gene sets with member gene lists, downloadable as GMT files.

**4. Biomart/Ensembl**
Query all GO annotations for rat in one programmatic call.

Option 2 or 3 is the most practical — a single bulk file gives you every GO term → gene mapping at once, no rate limiting. Want me to build a script that:

1. Downloads the rat GO annotation file
2. Cross-references your 2,800 GO terms
3. Outputs a mapping of GO ID → member genes
4. Cross-references those genes against our toxicogenomics consensus list?

That last step would be particularly interesting — it would show which of your GO clusters are enriched for the hallmark toxicogenomics genes we just identified.

<details><summary>Tool output (6017 chars)</summary>

```
     1→"""
     2→Build a GO term → gene member mapping and inner join with referenceUmapData.ts.
     3→
     4→Outputs a TSV: go_id, go_term, cluster_id, UMAP_1, UMAP_2, gene_symbol
     5→"""
     6→
     7→import csv
     8→import json
     9→import re
    10→from collections import defaultdict
    11→from pathlib import Path
    12→
    13→
    14→def parse_reference_umap(ts_path: str) -> list[dict]:
    15→    """Parse the TypeScript file to extract GO term data."""
    16→    with open(ts_path) as f:
    17→        text = f.read()
    18→
    19→    items = []
    20→    # Match each object block
    21→    pattern = re.compile(
    22→        r'\{\s*'
    23→        r'UMAP_1:\s*([-\d.]+),\s*'
    24→        r'UMAP_2:\s*([-\d.]+),\s*'
    25→        r'go_id:\s*"(GO:\d+)",\s*'
    26→        r'go_term:\s*"([^"]+)",\s*'
    27→        r'cluster_id:\s*([-\d]+|"[^"]+")'
    28→        r'\s*\}',
    29→        re.DOTALL
    30→    )
    31→    for m in pattern.finditer(text):
    32→        cluster_raw = m.group(5).strip('"')
    33→        items.append({
    34→            "UMAP_1": float(m.group(1)),
    35→            "UMAP_2": float(m.group(2)),
    36→            "go_id": m.group(3),
    37→            "go_term": m.group(4),
    38→            "cluster_id": cluster_raw,
    39→        })
    40→    return items
    41→
    42→
    43→def parse_gaf(gaf_path: str, aspect: str = "P") -> dict[str, set[str]]:
    44→    """
    45→    Parse a GAF (Gene Association Format) file.
    46→    Returns: {GO_ID: {gene_symbol, ...}}
    47→
    48→    GAF columns (tab-separated):
    49→    0:  DB
    50→    1:  DB_Object_ID
    51→    2:  DB_Object_Symbol  (gene symbol)
    52→    3:  Qualifier
    53→    4:  GO_ID
    54→    5:  DB:Reference
    55→    6:  Evidence Code
    56→    7:  With/From
    57→    8:  Aspect (P=Biological Process, F=Molecular Function, C=Cellular Component)
    58→    9:  DB_Object_Name
    59→    10: DB_Object_Synonym
    60→    11: DB_Object_Type
    61→    12: Taxon
    62→    13: Date
    63→    14: Assigned_By
    64→    """
    65→    go_genes: dict[str, set[str]] = defaultdict(set)
    66→
    67→    with open(gaf_path) as f:
    68→        for line in f:
    69→            if line.startswith("!"):
    70→                continue
    71→            parts = line.rstrip("\n").split("\t")
    72→            if len(parts) < 15:
    73→                continue
    74→
    75→            go_id = parts[4]
    76→            gene_symbol = parts[2]
    77→            line_aspect = parts[8]
    78→            qualifier = parts[3]
    79→
    80→            # Filter to Biological Process only
    81→            if aspect and line_aspect != aspect:
    82→                continue
    83→
    84→            # Skip negative annotations (NOT qualifiers)
    85→            if "NOT" in qualifier:
    86→                continue
    87→
    88→            go_genes[go_id].add(gene_symbol)
    89→
    90→    return dict(go_genes)
    91→

... (truncated)
```
</details>

There it is. The `rat_symbol` column shows the original rat GAF nomenclature:

- **`human`** rows: `rat_symbol` is empty

## User (2026-02-17T16:15:21.590Z)

what sort of thing is in the tox_evidence column?

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)



Three values, based on how many of our 798 toxicogenomics papers mentioned the gene:

| Value | Meaning | Count in lookup |
|-------|---------|----------------|
| `consensus` | 3+ papers | 35 genes (e.g. NFE2L2, TP53, BCL2) |
| `moderate` | 2 papers | 34 genes (e.g. CLU, CCNG1, EGR1) |
| `single` | 1 paper | 363 genes (e.g. BECN1, FOXM1, MAP3K8) |
| *(empty)* | Not found in any toxicogenomics paper | The remaining ~124k rows |

## User (2026-02-17T16:16:24.187Z)

write up our heuristic, what we did, and how we summarized the findings

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

> **Bash** — Gather stats for writeup
> ```
> uv run python3 -c "
import json, csv
from collections import Counter

# Gather stats for the writeup
papers = json.load(open('citegraph_output_800/papers.json'))
extractions = json.load(open('citegraph_output_800/extractions.json'))
consensus = json.load(open('citegraph_output/gene_consensus.json'))

with_abstract = sum(1 for e in extractions if e.get('raw_response'))
with_genes = sum(1 for e in extractions if e.get('genes'))

# TSV stats
tox_rows = 0
consensus_rows = 0
moderate_rows = 0
single_rows = 0
unique_go_with_tox = set()
with open('go_term_genes.tsv') as f:
    reader = csv.DictReader(f, delimiter='\t')
    for row in reader:
        if row['tox_evidence']:
            tox_rows += 1
            unique_go_with_tox.add(row['go_id'])
            if row['tox_evidence'] == 'consensus': consensus_rows += 1
            elif row['tox_evidence'] == 'moderate': moderate_rows += 1
            elif row['tox_evidence'] == 'single': single_rows += 1

print(f'papers_total={len(papers)}')
print(f'extractions_with_abstract={with_abstract}')
print(f'extractions_with_genes={with_genes}')

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

Here's the writeup:

---

## Methodology: Citation-Graph-Driven Toxicogenomics Gene Discovery

### Objective

Identify hallmark genes for organ-specific toxicity in rat, relevant to human health, by mining the scientific literature at scale and cross-referencing with Gene Ontology biological process annotations.

### Phase 1: Citation Graph Construction

**Approach.** Rather than manually curating papers, we built a citation graph outward from seed papers using the Semantic Scholar API, governed by relevance-scoring heuristics that control expansion.

**Seed selection.** 7 papers were selected by DOI as anchors spanning the field:
- Meier et al. 2024 — *Progress in toxicogenomics to protect human health* (Nature Reviews Genetics, comprehensive review, 193 references)
- TXG-MAP (2017) and TXG-MAPr (2021) — liver co-expression module frameworks
- Open TG-GATEs (2014) — the foundational toxicogenomics database
- TransTox (2024) — multi-organ transcriptomic translation (liver ↔ kidney)
- Reconciled rat-human metabolic networks (2017)
- MSigDB Hallmark Gene Sets (2015)

An additional 5 seed papers were added via keyword search targeting underrepresented organs (kidney, heart).

**Graph expansion.** From each seed, we fetched its references and citing papers from Semantic Scholar, then expanded outward to depth 2. Each candidate paper was scored for relevance before inclusion.

**Governor system.** Five stopping criteria controlled the crawl to prevent runaway expansion:

1. **Relevance threshold (0.3).** Each paper was scored 0.0–1.0 against 25 topic keywords (toxicogenomics, gene expression, transcriptomics, organ names, biomarker, etc.). Title matches were weighted 3× over abstract matches. Papers below 0.3 were discarded. Of ~3,000 papers considered, **2,208 were rejected** by this filter.

2. **Max depth (2).** No paper more than 2 citation hops from a seed was included, preventing drift into tangential fields.

3. **Max papers (800).** Hard budget cap. The crawl exhausted its queue at 798 papers before hitting this limit, indicating natural saturation of the relevance-filtered graph.

4. **Saturation detection.** A sliding window tracked the novelty of incoming papers by measuring new bigram concepts. If the last 20 papers contributed fewer than 5% new concepts, the crawl would stop. This did not trigger — the relevance filter was strict enough to maintain novelty.

5. **API budget (500 calls).** Hard cap on Semantic Scholar API requests. The crawl used 293.

**Result.** 798 papers, 999 citation edges, 326 reviews. Organ distribution: liver (60 papers at depth 0), kidney (40), heart (24), brain (20), lung (4), intestine (4), plus minor representation of adrenal, spleen, testis. The primary focus on liver and kidney reflects the seed selection; other organs were incidental catches via citation chains.

### Phase 2: LLM-Based Claim and Gene Extraction

**Approach.** Each paper's abstract was processed by a local LLM (Qwen 2.5 14B, Q4_K_M quantization) to extract structured information: gene names, organs studied, methods used, scientific claims, and a stance classification.

**Infrastructure.** Two GPUs ran in parallel:
- RTX 3080 Ti (12GB, local) — qwen2.5:14b via Ollama
- RX 6900 XT (16GB, remote via SSH tunnel) — qwen2.5:14b via Ollama

Papers were distributed round-robin across endpoints. No cloud LLM tokens were consumed.

**Extraction prompt.** The LLM was instructed to return a JSON object with fields for claims, genes (as standard symbols), organs, methods, species, a stance classification (supports_consensus / challenges_consensus / neutral / novel_finding), and a confidence score. A JSON parser with fallback heuristics handled formatting variations.

**Result.** 650 of 798 papers had abstracts available for extraction (148 lacked abstracts in Semantic Scholar). 172 papers yielded explicit gene names. This is expected — most papers discuss genes in full text, not abstracts.

### Phase 3: Gene Name Normalization

**Problem.** The same gene appears under different names across papers: P53 vs TP53, BCL-2 vs BCL2, TNF-α vs TNFΑ vs TNF, NRF2 vs NFE2L2, Caspase-3 vs CASP3, KIM-1 vs HAVCR1.

**Approach.** A curated alias table of ~100 mappings was built covering:
- Common name ↔ HGNC symbol (NRF2 → NFE2L2, KIM-1 → HAVCR1)
- Punctuation/casing variants (BCL-2 → BCL2, TNF-α → TNF)
- Protein names → gene symbols (Caspase-3 → CASP3)
- Greek letter variants (PPARα → PPARA, IL-1β → IL1B)

Organ names were similarly normalized (hippocampus/cerebellum/cortex → brain, hepatocytes → liver, etc.).

**Impact.** Normalization consolidated 461 raw gene names into 432 unique genes and significantly boosted consensus counts:
- TP53: 8 → 14 papers (merged P53 + TP53)
- BCL2: 4 → 11 papers (merged BCL-2 + BCL2)
- TNF: 4 → 8 papers (merged TNF-α + TNFΑ + TNF)
- HAVCR1: 0 → 4 papers (merged KIM-1 + KIM1)

### Phase 4: Consensus Classification

**Heuristic.** Genes were classified by the number of independent papers mentioning them:

- **Consensus (3+ papers):** 35 genes. These are established hallmark genes with cross-study validation. The top tier: NFE2L2 (27 papers), TP53 (14), BAX (12), PPARA (12), BCL2 (11), TNF (8), NFKB1 (7), CASP3 (7), IL6 (7).

- **Moderate evidence (2 papers):** 34 genes. Corroborated by at least one independent study. Includes CLU, CCNG1, EGR1, FGF21, KIM-1/HAVCR1, DRP1, GPX4.

- **Single mention (1 paper):** 363 genes. These include both well-known genes that happened to appear in only one abstract (undersampling artifact) and genuinely novel candidates from recent papers. Notably, several clusters of single-mention genes came from recent (2025–2026) single-cell sequencing and ML-based network studies, representing frontier candidates not yet validated by independent work.

**Organ-specific patterns.** The consensus genes partition into:
- Universal stress response: NFE2L2, BAX, TP53, BCL2 (appear across nearly all organs)
- Liver-specific: PPARA, AHR, CAR, CYP1A1, CYP7A1, GCLC, HSP90AA1
- Kidney-emerging: HAVCR1 (KIM-1), CLU, CD44, MDM2
- Heart: MFN2, SIRT1, IL1B, AMPK, TTN, DRP1 (mitochondrial dynamics)
- Cross-organ inflammation: TNF, NFKB1, IL6, IL1B

### Phase 5: GO Term Cross-Reference

**Approach.** 2,840 Gene Ontology Biological Process terms (from BMDExpress reference UMAP data, with cluster assignments) were cross-referenced with gene annotations from two sources:

1. **Rat GO annotations** (EBI GOA, goa_rat.gaf) — 12,363 GO terms, 564K annotations
2. **Human GO annotations** (EBI GOA, goa_human.gaf) — 11,207 GO terms, 834K annotations

An outer join was performed: every GO term appears in the output regardless of whether gene annotations exist. Each gene row is tagged with species provenance:
- `rat` — annotated only in rat GAF
- `human` — annotated only in human GAF
- `rat | human` — annotated in both (conserved ortholog with shared function)

For `rat` and `rat | human` rows, the original rat gene symbol is preserved in a separate column alongside the HGNC display symbol.

Each gene was then cross-referenced against the toxicogenomics consensus analysis, adding evidence level, paper count, and implicated organs.

**Result.** 130,699 rows covering all 2,840 GO terms. 2,391 terms had gene annotations (449 had none in either species). 6,074 rows matched a toxicogenomics gene, spanning 1,318 distinct GO biological processes. Of these, 1,629 rows matched consensus-level genes, 739 matched moderate-evidence genes, and 3,706 matched single-mention genes.

### Summary of Outputs

| File | Contents |
|------|----------|
| `citegraph_output_800/papers.json` | 798 papers with metadata, relevance scores, organ tags |
| `citegraph_output_800/edges.json` | 999 citation edges |
| `citegraph_output_800/citation_graph.gml` | Graph file for Gephi/networkx visualization |
| `citegraph_output_800/extractions.json` | 798 LLM extractions (claims, genes, organs, methods) |
| `citegraph_output/gene_consensus.json` | 432 genes classified by evidence level with organ mappings |
| `go_term_genes.tsv` | 130,699 rows: GO terms × genes × species × toxicogenomics evidence |

### Limitations

1. **Abstract-only extraction.** Gene names were extracted from abstracts, not full text. This undersamples genes discussed only in results/methods sections. The 172/650 yield rate (26%) reflects this limitation.

2. **Liver/kidney bias.** Seed paper selection focused on liver and kidney. Heart, brain, and lung gene coverage is incidental and incomplete. Dedicated crawls for these organs would improve coverage.

3. **Keyword-based relevance scoring.** The governor's relevance filter uses keyword matching, not semantic understanding. Papers using unusual terminology may be incorrectly filtered.

4. **Gene name normalization coverage.** The alias table covers ~100 common variants but is not exhaustive. Uncommon aliases or very recent gene name changes may be missed.

5. **No full-text or database validation.** The gene lists have not been cross-validated against curated databases (CTD, TXG-MAPr modules) or full-text extraction. This would strengthen the consensus classification significantly.

---

Want me to save this as a file in the project directory?

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

Each organ gets its own independent crawl with its own governor. The three crawls run sequentially (heart → brain → lung), and each one has:

- Its own paper budget (400 papers each)
- Its own depth limit (2 hops from *its* seeds)
- Its own saturation tracker
- Its own API call budget (500 calls each)
- Its own relevance scoring with organ-specific boost keywords (+0.15 for papers mentioning cardiac/brain/lung terms)

So a heart paper deep in the citation graph of a cardiac seed is at depth 1-2 from that seed, not depth 2+ from some unrelated liver paper. Each crawl builds a completely independent graph saved to its own directory (`citegraph_output_heart/`, `citegraph_output_brain/`, `citegraph_output_lung/`).

If we ran all organs in a single crawl, you'd have exactly the problem you're describing — heart papers would compete with liver papers for the 400-paper budget, and heart-specific papers at depth 2 from a cardiac seed would be at depth 4+ from the liver seeds, making them unreachable.

The rate limiting is slowing things down a bit. Let me check progress:

> ```

   214→class ParallelExtractionEngine:
   215→    """
   216→    Distributes paper extraction across multiple Ollama endpoints.
   217→    Each endpoint gets its own thread; papers are assigned round-robin.
   218→    """
   219→
   220→    def __init__(self, endpoints: list[OllamaEndpoint] | None = None):

   222→            endpoints = [LOCAL_OLLAMA, REMOTE_OLLAMA]
   223→
   224→        # Only keep endpoints that are reachable
   225→        self.endpoints = []
   226→        for ep in endpoints:
   227→            if ep.is_available():

   230→            else:

## User (2026-02-17T17:07:25.705Z)

the crawls had organ names, and other key words. what were they?

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

The crawls used three layers of keywords:

**1. Topic keywords** (shared by all crawls, used for relevance scoring):

```
toxicogenomics, toxicogenomic, gene expression, transcriptomics, transcriptomic,
rat, rattus, liver, hepat, kidney, renal, nephro, heart, cardiac, lung, pulmonary,
organ, tissue, hallmark, biomarker, signature, toxicity, toxic, adverse,
human health, translational, drug-induced, chemical exposure
```

Title matches weighted 3x over abstract matches.

**2. Organ tagging keywords** (used to tag papers by organ, shared by all crawls):

| Organ | Keywords |
|-------|----------|
| liver | liver, hepat, hepatocyte, hepatotoxic, hepatic |
| kidney | kidney, renal, nephro, nephrotoxic, tubular |
| heart | heart, cardiac, cardiotoxic, myocard, cardiovascular |
| lung | lung, pulmonary, pneumo, respiratory, alveol |
| brain | brain, neuro, cerebr, hippocamp, cortical |
| bone_marrow | bone marrow, hematopoiet, myelotoxic |
| intestine | intestin, gut, gastro, colonic, entero |
| spleen | spleen, splenic |
| muscle | muscle, myotoxic, skeletal muscle |
| thyroid | thyroid |
| adrenal | adrenal |
| testis | testis, testicular, spermat |

**3. Organ boost keywords** (new, +0.15 relevance bonus for organ-specific crawls):

| Organ | Boost keywords |
|-------|---------------|
| **Heart** | cardiac, cardiotoxic, cardiotoxicity, heart, myocardial, myocardium, cardiomyocyte, cardiomyopathy, doxorubicin, anthracycline, troponin, cardiovascular, arrhythm, qtc |
| **Brain** | brain, neurotoxic, neurotoxicity, neurotoxicogenomics, neurodegeneration, neurodegenerative, cerebral, cortical, hippocampal, hippocampus, dopaminergic, serotonergic, glutamatergic, cns, blood-brain, astrocyte, microglia, neuron, seizure, encephalopathy |
| **Lung** | lung, pulmonary, respiratory, inhalation, pneumotoxic, alveolar, bronchial, bronchiolar, fibrosis, pneumonitis, nanoparticle, nanotube, aerosol, surfactant, clara cell, type ii pneumocyte |

Papers matching 2+ boost keywords get +0.15; 1 match gets +0.08. This helps organ-relevant papers pass the 0.3 relevance threshold even if they don't hit many general toxicogenomics terms.

## User (2026-02-17T17:09:09.398Z)

i'm thinking that some of the terms might be limiting. like toxicogenomics, genomics, etc. can you explain how the crawl works, base on the search terms?

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

The keywords don't control *which papers are searched for* — they control *which papers survive the relevance filter* once found through citation chains. Here's the full flow:

**Step 1: Seeds (no keyword filtering)**
Seed papers are added by DOI or search query. They get relevance = 1.0 automatically, regardless of keywords.

**Step 2: Citation expansion (no keyword filtering)**
From each seed, we fetch its references and citing papers from Semantic Scholar. This is purely structural — we follow citation links, not keywords.

**Step 3: Relevance gate (keywords matter here)**
Each candidate paper discovered via citations gets scored against the 25 topic keywords. The formula:

```
raw = (title_hits × 3 + abstract_hits) / (25 × 4)
score = raw × 3   (capped at 1.0)
```

To pass the 0.3 threshold, a paper needs roughly:
- 2 title keyword hits + 4 abstract hits, OR
- 0 title hits + 10 abstract hits, OR  
- 3 title hits + 1 abstract hit

**The concern you're raising is real but maybe less severe than it seems.** The keyword list includes very broad terms: "rat", "heart", "cardiac", "gene expression", "organ", "tissue", "toxic", "drug-induced", "biomarker". A typical cardiac paper that says "doxorubicin-induced cardiac injury in rats with gene expression changes" hits ~6 keywords without ever saying "toxicogenomics."

**Where it does bite:** papers using unusual language — e.g., pure pharmacology papers about drug mechanisms, or clinical cardiology papers that discuss gene variants but don't use tox/genomics vocabulary. These could be relevant but get filtered at 0.3.

The original general crawl rejected 2,208 papers this way. The heart crawl rejected 450, brain rejected 858, lung rejected 630.

Want me to loosen the threshold, broaden the keyword list, or take a different approach entirely?

## User (2026-02-17T17:11:24.872Z)

i'm wondering what we're missing if we throw away gene function papers that have nothing to do with genomics.

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

That's a sharp observation. Right now we're collecting papers that say "gene X changes during toxicity" — the toxicogenomics layer. But we're missing the layer underneath: papers that explain *what gene X actually does* in that organ.

For example, our consensus genes include:

- **MFN2** (heart) — we know it appears in cardiotoxicity papers, but we're missing the mitochondrial fusion biology papers that explain *why* it matters for cardiomyocytes
- **HAVCR1/KIM-1** (kidney) — we caught it as a toxicity marker, but the nephrology papers explaining its role as a proximal tubule injury sensor got filtered out
- **NFE2L2/NRF2** (universal) — the oxidative stress biology literature is enormous and mostly doesn't say "toxicogenomics"
- **BDNF, SLC6A3** (brain) — neuroscience papers about their synaptic function wouldn't pass our filter

We're essentially building the "what changes" map but missing the "what it does" map. The GO annotations from the GAF files partially fill this gap — they tell us NFE2L2 is involved in "response to oxidative stress" — but they don't capture the organ-specific mechanistic detail that papers would.

There are a few ways to address this:

1. **Gene-function crawl**: Take our consensus/moderate genes and run a second-pass crawl per organ with completely different keywords — gene names + organ terms, no tox vocabulary required
2. **Lean on GO + UniProt**: The functional annotation is already partially captured in our GO cross-reference. We could enrich it further with pathway databases (KEGG, Reactome)
3. **Hybrid scoring**: Add a second relevance path — if a paper mentions a known consensus gene by name AND an organ, let it through regardless of tox keywords

Which direction interests you?

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

Good question — let me give an honest assessment of the three scripts while the plan agent finishes.

**Overall structure**: The three scripts form a clear pipeline (`citegraph.py` → `extract.py` → `go_gene_map.py`), each handling one phase. That's clean separation.

**What's nice:**
- Governor pattern in `citegraph.py` is well-designed — 5 independent stopping criteria in a composable config dataclass
- The `S2Client` retry logic with backoff is robust
- `ParallelExtractionEngine` cleanly handles GPU discovery, weighted distribution, thread-safe stats
- Gene normalization is centralized in one alias table

**What's not DRY / hardcoded:**
- **Hardcoded paths everywhere**: `citegraph_output_800/papers.json`, `/home/svobodadl/Dev/Projects/BMDExpress-Web-Edition/...`, `/tmp/goa_rat.gaf` — all absolute paths baked into `__main__` blocks
- **Organ keywords duplicated**: `citegraph.py` has organ_keywords for tagging AND organ_boost_keywords per crawl AND topic_keywords that also contain organ terms. Three overlapping lists for related purposes.
- **Normalization not shared cleanly**: `go_gene_map.py` imports `normalize_gene` from `extract.py` via `sys.path.insert(0, ".")` — fragile hack
- **CLI parsing is ad-hoc**: Each script hand-parses `sys.argv` differently. No argparse, no consistent interface.
- **No config file**: Paths, endpoints, thresholds all hardcoded in Python. Should be a config file or at minimum a shared constants module.
- **`GENE_ALIASES` is a flat dict in extract.py**: Works but not maintainable at scale — should probably be a TSV/JSON file loaded at runtime
- **Seed papers hardcoded in functions**: `run_toxicogenomics_crawl()` and `ORGAN_CRAWL_CONFIGS` embed DOIs directly in code

**Verdict**: Functional research code, not production code. The algorithms are sound but the infrastructure is held together with hardcoded paths and ad-hoc CLI parsing. Good enough for the research task, would need cleanup before anyone else used it.

I'll fold improvements into the plan where they make sense (especially the shared config and cleaner imports). Let me check if the plan agent is done:

## Assistant (claude-opus-4-6)

Papers that appear in both the tox crawl and the gene-function crawl should be **kept but deduplicated**. Here's why:

- **Don't discard them** — a paper appearing in both crawls is actually *more* valuable, not less. It means the paper sits at the intersection of toxicogenomics and gene function, exactly the kind of paper that bridges the two knowledge layers.

- **Don't double-count them** — when merging consensus across all crawls, each paper should only count once per gene. If paper X mentions TP53 and appears in both the tox crawl and the genefunc crawl, TP53 still gets +1, not +2.

The `merge_gene_consensus()` function handles this by deduplicating on `paper_id` before counting. The merged result will have:
- Same gene counted once per unique paper regardless of which crawl(s) found it
- A `sources` tag showing where each paper came from (e.g., `["tox_800", "organ_heart", "genefunc"]`)

This means the gene-function crawl can only *increase* consensus counts (by finding new papers not in the tox crawl), never inflate existing ones. In practice, most gene-function papers should be new — the tox crawl filtered them out precisely because they lacked tox vocabulary.

Want me to update the plan with this clarification and proceed, or any other questions?
